    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="fb57726da9a146d1ac7bc182f26c6e7c">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '187bf7a4-6200-48ff-bfbb-5fd5511b7429'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d52995297" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>The practical application of sodium (Na) metal anode has been seriously hindered by the uncontrolled Na dendrite growth and severe volume expansion inducing short battery lifespan and safety concerns. Here, highly sodiophilic and ultrafine ZnS-modified carbon fibers have been rationally constructed as a scaffold to obviate these drawbacks. The in situ generated Na<sub>2</sub>S-reinforced fast ion transport interphase and the effective space confinement synergistically facilitate the fast sodium ion interfacial transfer and the homogeneous and dendrite-free Na deposition, thus enabling high-rate and dendrite-free Na metal anode. The modified Na metal demonstrates ultrahigh rate capability (15&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup>) and ultralong cycling life (5300 cycles). Moreover, the Na|Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (NVP) cell delivers ultrahigh rate capability (80 C) and extraordinarily ultralong lifespan cycling durability (2980 cycles). The Na|NVP pouch cell also exhibits an extraordinarily ultralong-term cycle life of over 1070 cycles with an extremely low capacity decay rate of 0.0088% per cycle at 10 C. This work provides a facile and efficient strategy to propel the Na metal anode towards practicability.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202314954-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202314954-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>The ever-rising demand for high energy density for renewable energy storage market such as electric transportation and smart grids needs the exploration of reliable and advanced energy storage beyond conventional lithium-ion batteries.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314954-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314954-bib-0001_R_d52995289e451" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Energy Environ. Sci.">1</a>, <a href="#adfm202314954-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314954-bib-0002_R_d52995289e451" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Chem">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Notably, Na resource shows the merits of abundant reserve and low cost.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314954-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314954-bib-0003_R_d52995289e456" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Energy Environ. Sci.">3</a>-<a href="#adfm202314954-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314954-bib-0005_R_d52995289e456" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Science">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Utilizing the Na metal anode could promote a cost-effective and high-energy energy storage due to its high specific capacity (≈1165 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) and low electrochemical potential (−2.71&nbsp;V vs the standard hydrogen electrode).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314954-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314954-bib-0003_R_d52995289e464" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Energy Environ. Sci.">3</a>, <a href="#adfm202314954-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314954-bib-0006_R_d52995289e464" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Chem. Soc. Rev.">6</a>, <a href="#adfm202314954-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314954-bib-0007_R_d52995289e464" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Chem. Rev.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, sodium (Na) metal batteries (SMBs) have been recognized as one of the most promising alternatives to lithium-ion batteries because Na shows properties similar to those of Li but costs less. Nevertheless, the practical application of Na metal anode has seriously been hindered by the uncontrollable Na metal electrodeposition during the repeated Na plating/stripping cycling process, inducing mossy or dendritic Na metal growth, the cell short circuit, and even serious safety hazards.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314954-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314954-bib-0008_R_d52995289e469" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Nat. Commun.">8</a>, <a href="#adfm202314954-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314954-bib-0009_R_d52995289e469" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Nano Lett.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Due to the high reactivity of Na metal, a solid-electrolyte-interphase (SEI) layer will form on the Na metal surface. Repeated collapse and reformation of the SEI layer will occur during the repeated cycling owing to its fragile nature, inducing low Coulombic efficiency and poor cell cycling performance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314954-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314954-bib-0010_R_d52995289e474" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In addition, the massive volume expansion of Na metal during the repeated charging/discharging process will also rupture the SEI layer, leading to fast cell deterioration.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314954-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314954-bib-0011_R_d52995289e480" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Nano Lett.">11</a>, <a href="#adfm202314954-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314954-bib-0012_R_d52995289e480" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Nano Energy">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, constructing stable and dendrite-free Na metal is highly desirable to promote the development of reliable and practical SMBs.</p>
         
         <p>Tremendous strategies have been proposed to stabilize and optimize the Na metal anode, including electrolyte modulation,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314954-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314954-bib-0013_R_d52995289e488" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Energy Environ. Sci.">13</a>, <a href="#adfm202314954-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314954-bib-0014_R_d52995289e488" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Small Methods">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> artificial SEI protective layer,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314954-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314954-bib-0015_R_d52995289e493" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Mater. Adv.">15</a>-<a href="#adfm202314954-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314954-bib-0017_R_d52995289e493" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Adv. Energy Mater.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> solid electrolytes,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314954-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314954-bib-0018_R_d52995289e498" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - J. Energy Chem.">18</a>, <a href="#adfm202314954-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314954-bib-0019_R_d52995289e498" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Adv. Energy Mater.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> 3D host,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314954-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314954-bib-0020_R_d52995289e504" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Energy Storage Mater.">20</a>-<a href="#adfm202314954-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314954-bib-0022_R_d52995289e504" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - ACS Nano">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and so on. Particularly, Na<sub>2</sub>S has been reported as highly efficient in inhibiting Na dendrite growth for its high ionic conductivity and low Na<sup>+</sup> migration energy barrier.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314954-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314954-bib-0023_R_d52995289e514" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Adv. Mater.">23</a>, <a href="#adfm202314954-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314954-bib-0024_R_d52995289e514" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Nano Lett.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Meanwhile, carbonaceous host shows the superiorities of high electronic conductivity, low density, and low cost, thus showing a promising prospect for large-scale application.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314954-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314954-bib-0025_R_d52995289e519" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">25</a>-<a href="#adfm202314954-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314954-bib-0027_R_d52995289e519" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Adv. Sci.">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It is worth noting that the cycling durability of Na metal anode remains practically difficult under harsh conditions of high capacity/current density of &gt; 5&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup> and 5&nbsp;mA h cm<sup>−2</sup>. One reason is that it is difficult to obviate the massive volume variation especially when encountered with high current density and large plating capacity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314954-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314954-bib-0028_R_d52995289e529" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Adv. Energy Mater.">28</a>-<a href="#adfm202314954-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314954-bib-0030_R_d52995289e529" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Nano Lett.">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Another is the instability of the native solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) on the Na metal surface.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314954-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314954-bib-0031_R_d52995289e534" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The repeated SEI layer collapse/reformation during the cycling process will induce a fast depletion of Na metal/electrolyte and a gradual increase of interfacial charge transfer resistance. Specifically, the substantial volume variation of Na metal will exacerbate the SEI layer rupture.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314954-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314954-bib-0032_R_d52995289e539" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Adv. Mater.">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, constructing a mechanically stable Na metal anode with reinforced SEI layer is highly desirable to retard Na dendrite growth and foster the development of reliable Na metal batteries. Here, the space-confined Na metal anode with reinforced SEI layer has been facilely achieved with the robust ultrafine ZnS-modified carbon fibers (CF@ZnS) host. The in situ generated Na<sub>2</sub>S-reinforced interphase layer and the effective space confinement are conducive to fast sodium ion interfacial transfer, highly reversible Na plating/stripping, and homogeneous metallic Na nucleation/growth. Consequently, the modified Na metal anode demonstrates high rate capability (15&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup>) and ultra-long lifespan cycling stability (5300 cycles). Particularly, an ultralong cycling life of over 1390&nbsp;h still can be achieved at the harsh test conditions of 5&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup> and 5 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>. The Na|Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (NVP) full cells show ultrahigh rate performance (80 C) and extraordinarily ultralong lifespan cycling durability for 2980 cycles. Furthermore, the Na|NVP pouch cell also exhibits an extraordinarily ultralong-term cycling sustainability over 1070 cycles with an extremely low decay rate of 0.0088% per cycle even at 10 C. This work provides a new cost-effective and large-scale avenue toward highly stable and reliable Na metal batteries.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202314954-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202314954-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>The ultrafine ZnS modified carbon fibers (CF@ZnS) were prepared from the self-assembly of metal-organic frameworks followed by the sulfurization treatment, which is then utilized as a host to construct the robust CF@ZnS@Na electrode. The in situ-generated Na<sub>2</sub>S-reinforced SEI layer and NaZn<sub>13</sub> alloy nanoseeds can enable uniform Na nucleation/growth and highly reversible Na plating/stripping. <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202314954-fig-0001">1a</a> schematically illustrates the preparation procedure of the CF@ZnS host. The combination of ZIF-8 precursor and the subsequent sulfurization treatment affords the highly efficient ultrafine ZnS active sites on carbon fiber host. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314954-fig-0001">1b</a> demonstrates the Na deposition mechanism on different hosts. For the bare CF host, notorious dendritic Na metal growth can be expected during the repeated Na plating/stripping process due to the sodiophobicity of the CF host and the instability of the natively formed SEI layer. As a sharp contrast, robust CF@ZnS@Na electrode with Na<sub>2</sub>S-reinforced SEI layer and inlaid ultrafine NaZn<sub>13</sub> active seeds can effectively modulate the homogenized Na<sup>+</sup> flux and uniform Na nucleation/growth, thus enabling the uniform Na deposition. Moreover, the large volume expansion of the Na metal anode can be effectively mitigated due to the space confinement from the high surface area and porous architecture of the CF@ZnS@Na.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202314954-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/c2ec5c5d-3c73-4993-81e1-403103791746/adfm202314954-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/c2ec5c5d-3c73-4993-81e1-403103791746/adfm202314954-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/86b68faa-7cf6-4af4-9582-413e3d4ba772/adfm202314954-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/c2ec5c5d-3c73-4993-81e1-403103791746/adfm202314954-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202314954-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202314954" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Schematic illustration of the preparation of CF@ZnS and the Na deposition mechanism. a) The preparation process of CF@ZnS. b) The Na deposition mechanism on bare CF and CF@ZnS.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <div class="paragraph-element">The ultrafine ZnS modification on CF was obtained via the ZIF-8 self-assembly. The X-ray diffraction (XRD) pattern reveals the successful preparation of the ZIF-8 precursor (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2a</a>, Supporting Information, the as-prepared ZIF-8 shows uniform nanocube morphology with an average size of ≈60&nbsp;nm, and there is no obvious morphology change after the sulfurization treatment (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2b</a>, Supporting Information). Followed by the sulfurization and the subsequent pyrolysis treatment, an ultrafine ZnS-modified carbon fibers host can be eventually obtained. While the XRD patterns of the as-prepared CF@ZnS only show two broad peaks centered at 25<sup>o</sup> and 43<sup>o</sup> corresponding to the characteristic diffractions of amorphous carbon. The absence of ZnS peaks can be ascribed to the extremely low loading amount of ZnS and its ultrahigh dispersion (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202314954-fig-0002">2a</a>). In contrast, pure ZnS without CF host was also prepared adopting the same preparation procedure. The obvious and strong characteristic peaks can be designated to ZnS (JCPDS 36–1450), which can firmly verify the successful synthesis of ZnS. In addition, the X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) characterization of CF@ZnS was also conducted. The obvious 2p<sub>1/2</sub> and 2p<sub>3/2</sub> peaks designated to S<sup>2−</sup> can be detected in the S 2p spectrum (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314954-fig-0002">2b</a>). Together with the obvious Zn 2p XPS spectrum (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314954-fig-0002">2c</a>), it can further firmly prove the formation of ZnS. The morphology of CF@ZnS was also examined by the SEM technique. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314954-fig-0002">2d</a>, uniformly dispersed ultrafine ZnS can be clearly observed wrapping around the carbon fibers host. The corresponding elemental mapping reveals the ultrahigh dispersion of ZnS (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information). The ultrahigh dispersion can be ascribed to the effective stabilization from the CF host.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314954-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314954-bib-0033_R_d52995289e652" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Small">33</a>, <a href="#adfm202314954-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314954-bib-0034_R_d52995289e652" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314954-fig-0002">2e,f</a> shows the TEM image of CF@ZnS. It can be detected that the ultrafine ZnS nanoparticles are uniformly distributed and coated on the carbon fibers with an average size of ≈9&nbsp;nm. The highly dispersed and sodiophilic ZnS nanoseeds can effectively enhance the compatibility of CF with Na metal. Upon the sodiation process, ZnS could be in situ transformed into Na<sub>2</sub>S and NaZn<sub>13</sub>. And the cyclic voltammetry measurement of the sodiation/desodiation of CF@ZnS can verify this hypothesis. The peak at 0.18&nbsp;V can be designated to the alloying reaction of Na and Zn while the one at 0.73&nbsp;V corresponds to the dealloying of NaZn<sub>13</sub> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314954-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314954-bib-0004_R_d52995289e670" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Energy Environ. Sci.">4</a>, <a href="#adfm202314954-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314954-bib-0035_R_d52995289e670" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Nano Energy">35</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It is concluded therefore that ZnS was in situ transformed into NaZn<sub>13</sub> in the CF@ZnS@Na electrode. The transformation reaction of ZnS can be described as the following equation:

            <div class="inline-equation" id="adfm202314954-disp-0001"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                  <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/aa68e673-2bc5-4245-93ad-0ec17038d03b/adfm202314954-math-0001.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:1616301X:media:adfm202314954:adfm202314954-math-0001" display="block" location="graphic/adfm202314954-math-0001.png">
                     <semantics>
                        <mrow>
                           <mi>ZnS</mi>
                           <mo linebreak="badbreak">+</mo>
                           <mi>Na</mi>
                           <mo>→</mo>
                           <msub>
                              <mi>Na</mi>
                              <mn>2</mn>
                           </msub>
                           <mi mathvariant="normal">S</mi>
                           <mo linebreak="goodbreak">+</mo>
                           <msub>
                              <mi>NaZn</mi>
                              <mn>13</mn>
                           </msub>
                        </mrow>
                        <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation}{\mathrm{ZnS}} + {\mathrm{Na}} \to {\mathrm{ Na}}_2{\mathrm{S}} + {\mathrm{NaZn}}_{13}\end{equation}$$</annotation>
                     </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(1)</span></div>
         </div>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202314954-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/5430bd86-898b-4922-973f-b75ad3ca4355/adfm202314954-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/5430bd86-898b-4922-973f-b75ad3ca4355/adfm202314954-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/eb79561b-0fc4-4d40-ba03-f6b9b54c693d/adfm202314954-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/5430bd86-898b-4922-973f-b75ad3ca4355/adfm202314954-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202314954-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202314954" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Phase structure and morphology characterizations of CF@ZnS. a) XRD patterns of the as-synthesized samples. High-resolution XPS spectra of b) S 2p and c) Zn 2p spectra of CF@ZnS. d) SEM image of CF@ZnS. e,f) TEM images of CF@ZnS.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>To systematically investigate the Na deposition behavior on different hosts, the Na|CF@ZnS and Na|CF half-cells were assembled. With a Na plating capacity of 3 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup> at the current density of 1&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup>, the bare CF surface manifests loose, random, and inhomogeneous Na deposition aggregation on the CF surface due to its sodiophobic nature (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202314954-fig-0003">3a</a>). As a sharp contrast, the CF@ZnS host demonstrates dense and uniform Na deposition tightly wrapping around the carbon fibers owing to the effective guidance from the NaZn<sub>13</sub> active seeds (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314954-fig-0003">3d</a>). The homogeneous Na deposition around CF@ZnS can also be obviously observed from the in situ optical images (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information). Upon increasing the Na deposition capacity, the fiber diameter increases gradually from 11.7 (3 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>) to 15.0&nbsp;µm (5 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314954-fig-0003">3e</a>). With further increasing the Na plating capacity, the Na metal still preferentially deposited along the fibers with obviously expanded diameter and no dendritic Na metal growth (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314954-fig-0003">3f</a>). The thickness of the CF@ZnS host with different Na plating capacities was also investigated to examine the mechanical stability of the CF@ZnS@Na. As exhibited, no obvious thickness variation can be observed for CF@ZnS@Na even when with a large Na plating capacity of 10 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314954-fig-0003">3j–l</a>). By comparison, a large thickness variation of the bare CF host can be observed (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314954-fig-0003">3g–i</a>). Na metal preferably deposits in the voids and the accumulated Na metal obviously detected stacking on the surface of bare CF due to its high sodiophobicity (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314954-fig-0003">3a–c,g–i</a>). The thickness of bare CF with a Na metal plating capacity of 3 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup> is about 257&nbsp;µm (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314954-fig-0003">3g</a>). Upon the increase of Na deposition capacity, the thickness of the electrode rises to ≈269 (5 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314954-fig-0003">3h</a>) and then further to ≈316&nbsp;µm (10 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314954-fig-0003">3i</a>), delivering a significant volume expansion during the Na plating process. In contrast, the CF@ZnS host shows quite a stable structure with no obvious thickness change during the whole Na plating process (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314954-fig-0003">3j–l</a>), manifesting the highly stabilized mechanical structure owing to its highly porous and high surface area 3D architecture.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202314954-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/d41e7ca2-f311-4739-b6b1-15cbe9cb2d74/adfm202314954-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/d41e7ca2-f311-4739-b6b1-15cbe9cb2d74/adfm202314954-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/062b9848-0ab9-4538-a5a8-62289856af0b/adfm202314954-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/d41e7ca2-f311-4739-b6b1-15cbe9cb2d74/adfm202314954-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202314954-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202314954" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Morphology characterization of the CF@ZnS and bare CF with different Na plating capacities. Top-view SEM images of the bare CF host with a Na plating capacity of a) 3, b) 5, and c) 10 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>. Top-view SEM images of the CF@ZnS with a Na plating capacity of d) 3, e) 5, and f) 10 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>. Cross-sectional SEM images of g–i) bare CF and j–l) CF@ZnS with different Na plating capacities.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>To prove the viability of CF@ZnS@Na for SMBs, its long-term galvanostatic cycling performance was first evaluated in Na symmetric cells. <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202314954-fig-0004">4a</a> shows the galvanostatic cycling performance of Na symmetric cells at the current density of 5&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup> with a capacity of 1 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>. As exhibited, the cell with CF@ZnS@Na anode demonstrates an impressively long-term cycling stability over 4280 cycles with a low polarization voltage of ≈20&nbsp;mV (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>, Supporting Information). In sharp comparison, the control cell with plain Na foil shows a large polarization overpotential and deteriorates fast with fluctuated voltage due to the serious Na dendrite growth. With the current density increasing to 10&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup>, an ultralong lifespan cycling durability over 5300 cycles still can be achieved for the cell with CF@ZnS@Na anode, which is in sharp contrast to the obvious voltage fluctuation and limited cycle life for the control cell with plain Na foil (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314954-fig-0004">4b</a>). Moreover, the cycling performance of CF@ZnS@Na with a large Na plating/stripping capacity was also tested. Remarkably, the cell with a modified Na metal anode manifests an ultralong lifespan stable operation over 1390&nbsp;h at a high current density of 5&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup> with a large capacity of 5 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314954-fig-0004">4c</a>). In addition, the rate performance of the Na symmetric cell was also tested (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a>, Supporting Information). The symmetric cell with CF@ZnS@Na exhibits stable cycling under the successively enlarged current densities. Impressively, the cell could still stably cycle even when encountered with an ultrahigh current density of 15&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup>. Stable cycling could also be maintained when the current density returns to 1&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup>. To provide a clear and intuitive elucidation for the excellent cycling performance CF@ZnS@Na in our work, the electrochemical cycling performance comparison with other reported works is carried out (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314954-fig-0004">4d</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314954-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314954-bib-0036_R_d52995289e848" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Adv. Energy Mater.">36</a>-<a href="#adfm202314954-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314954-bib-0045_R_d52995289e848" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - Chem. Eng. J.">45</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As exhibited, the cell with CF@ZnS@Na demonstrates an extraordinarily long lifespan cycling durability with low polarization voltage even when enduring a large Na plating capacity of 5 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>, significantly outperforming the previously reported works both in the cycling life and polarization overpotential. To disclose the superior electrochemical performance of CF@ZnS@Na, XPS analysis of the cycled CF@ZnS@Na has been conducted (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a>, Supporting Information). For the Na 1s spectra, the peak at 1073, 1071.8, 1071.5, and 1071&nbsp;eV could be attributed to signals of Na<sub>2</sub>S, NaF, Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>, and Na<sub>2</sub>O, respectively (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8a,b</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314954-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314954-bib-0023_R_d52995289e871" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Adv. Mater.">23</a>, <a href="#adfm202314954-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314954-bib-0046_R_d52995289e871" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">46</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Compared with that with plain Na foil, the appearance of the Na<sub>2</sub>S and its strong signal could firmly verify the formation of the Na<sub>2</sub>S-reinforced SEI layer on the cycled CF@ZnS@Na surface. In addition, the F 1s spectra can also validate our deduction, which shows two peaks at 687.2 and 683.7&nbsp;eV assigned to C–F and NaF (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8c,d</a>, Supporting Information). The much lower content of NaF in the SEI layer of cycled CF@ZnS@Na resulting from the reaction of Na metal and sodium salt could provide firm evidence for the significantly reduced side reaction of CF@ZnS@Na with electrolyte benefiting from the in situ-generated Na<sub>2</sub>S-reinforced SEI layer on CF@ZnS@Na surface. In addition, the electrochemical impendence spectroscopy evolution of Na symmetric cells upon cycling was also collected. As displayed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314954-fig-0004">4e,f</a>, the semicircle observed at high frequency can be attributed to the interfacial resistance associated with the passivation layer on the Na metal surface.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314954-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314954-bib-0047_R_d52995289e889" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - ACS Nano">47</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The fitted curve is shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a>, Supporting Information. Cell employing CF@ZnS@Na exhibits much lower and more stable interfacial resistance in comparison with that using bare Na foil, underscoring the substantial enhancement in charge transfer kinetics and strengthened electrode/electrolyte interface owing to the robust Na<sub>2</sub>S-reinforced SEI layer. Meanwhile, the influence of CF@ZnS on the Na deposition behavior during the cycling was also investigated. As demonstrated, smooth and compact Na deposition on the cycled CF@ZnS@Na surface can be observed, while serious Na dendrite growth was detected on the bare Na foil surface (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314954-fig-0004">4g,h</a>). Meanwhile, the Na deposition behavior on different Na metal anodes was also observed by the in situ optical microscopy (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a>, Supporting Information). As shown, a smooth surface can be detected on both the fresh Na foil and CF@ZnS@Na surface. However, serious Na dendrite growth on the plain Na foil was observed during the Na plating process. While smooth Na metal deposition on the CF@ZnS@Na surface can be kept even with a large Na plating capacity of 1 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>, revealing the significantly stabilized Na deposition behavior on the CF@ZnS@Na electrode. The homogenized Na deposition on CF@ZnS@Na can be attributed to the Na<sub>2</sub>S-enriched SEI layer and the highly efficient NaZn<sub>13</sub> seeds. Also, the exchange current density was also calculated from the Tafel plot (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314954-fig-0004">4i</a>). As exhibited, the cell with CF@ZnS@Na shows a much higher exchange current density of 0.33&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup>, revealing the significantly enhanced charge transfer kinetics owing to the robust Na<sub>2</sub>S-reinforced SEI layer. Also, the nucleation overpotential was also examined and the result is shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a>, Supporting Information. Much larger nucleation overpotential can be detected on both bare Cu (≈35&nbsp;mV) and bare CF (≈22&nbsp;mV), while a significantly minimized Na metal nucleation overpotential (≈12&nbsp;mV) can be detected on CF@ZnS surface, which can be ascribed to the effective regulation from the NaZn<sub>13</sub> nanoseeds and the Na<sub>2</sub>S-reinforced SEI layer. Also, the Coulombic efficiency (CE) of Na stripping to plating capacity ratio under different current densities was examined. At a high current density of 5&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup> with a capacity of 1 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>, CF@ZnS@Na shows a high average CE of 99.97% for as long as 2830 cycles (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314954-fig-0004">4j</a>), manifesting a superior high Na plating and stripping reversibility. By comparison, the cells with plain Na foil or bare CF-hosted Na metal show fluctuated CE with an extremely limited cycling lifespan. Also, the CE under a large Na plating capacity was also tested. At the current density of 3&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup> with a large Na plating capacity of 3 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>, the CE could be retained above 99.91% for over 1060 cycles while the control cells with plain Na foil or bare CF scaffolded Na degrades rapidly (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314954-fig-0004">4k</a>). When encountered with a high current density of 5&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup> and a large capacity of 5 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>, the modified Na anode also displays a superior average Coulombic efficiency of 99.94% over 250 cycles (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a>, Supporting Information). The high CE of CF@ZnS@Na indicates the stable and high Na plating/stripping reversibility on CF@ZnS owing to the synergy of the robust Na<sub>2</sub>S-reinforced SEI layer and highly efficient NaZn<sub>13</sub> active seeds.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202314954-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/4f80ba2e-6c0b-4e67-ad72-9985ce7f0c8a/adfm202314954-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/4f80ba2e-6c0b-4e67-ad72-9985ce7f0c8a/adfm202314954-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/f7f88706-b764-4f36-b5ee-57f608f25d18/adfm202314954-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/4f80ba2e-6c0b-4e67-ad72-9985ce7f0c8a/adfm202314954-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202314954-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202314954" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">The electrochemical performance of CF@ZnS@Na anode. a–c) The galvanostatic cycling performance of Na symmetric cells at different current densities with various Na plating/stripping capacities. d) The electrochemical performance comparison with the literature reports at different current densities with a Na plating capacity of 5 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>. e,f) The EIS curves of fresh and cycled cells at 5&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup> and 1 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>. The SEM images of g) CF@ZnS@Na and h) bare Na foil after 10 cycles at 5&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup> with a capacity of 1 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>. i) Tafel plots to calculate the exchange current density. The Coulombic efficiencies of different Na metal anodes at j) 5&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup>, 1 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup> and k) 3&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup>, 3 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>To further evaluate the viability of CF@ZnS@Na, the full cell coupled with Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (NVP) cathode was assembled and tested. As demonstrated, the Na|NVP cell with CF@ZnS@Na demonstrates superior rate performance with much higher discharge capacities of 114.1, 108.8, 104.7, 99.0, 90.4, and 76.9 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 1, 10, 20, 30, 50, and 80 C, in sharp comparison with those of 109.4, 100.3, 88.9, 77.6, 71.8, and 54.3 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> for the cells with plain Na foil (1C = 117 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202314954-fig-0005">5a,b</a>). The voltage-capacity profile of the Na|NVP cell is shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a>, Supporting Information. Much smaller voltage polarization can be detected for the cell with CF@ZnS@Na. During the long-term cycling at an ultrahigh current density of 20 C, CF@ZnS@Na|NVP cell also delivers an exceptional ultralong cycling durability over 2980 cycles with a high capacity retention of 86%, which is in sharp comparison to the fast capacity decay for the counterpart with plain Na foil (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314954-fig-0005">5c</a>). Notably, employing a thick cathode with high mass loading and strict control of the amount of Na metal is the ultimate avenue toward practical high-energy Na metal batteries. However, the currently reported high-performance Na metal batteries heavily rely on excessive Na metal, which inevitably induces a substantial compromise in the whole cell energy density. To promote the practical utilization of CF@ZnS@Na, strictly controlling the negative-to-positive capacity (N/P) ratio is of great importance. Thus, the electrochemical cycling performance of Na|NVP cell with thick cathode and anode-less conditions was also tested. Impressively, CF@ZnS@Na|NVP full cell manifests a long-term cycling stability over 430 cycles with a superior capacity retention of 83.1% even when subjected to a low N/P ratio of 2.3, while the control cell with plain Na metal shows only dozens of cycles when encountered with a relatively high N/P ratio of 9 (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314954-fig-0005">5d</a>). Furthermore, to further disclose the practical usage prospect, the corresponding pouch cell was also assembled. As noticeable, Na metal pouch cells are rarely reported or achieved in the most recently reported works. Impressively, the Na|NVP pouch cell shows an exceptionally ultralong lifespan cycling stability over 1070 cycles at 10 C with an extremely low-capacity decay rate of 0.0088% per cycle (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314954-fig-0005">5e</a>). Moreover, the Na|NVP pouch cell with ultrathick cathode (≈12&nbsp;mg cm<sup>−2</sup>) also exhibits long-term cycling stability over 200 cycles at 1 C with a superior capacity retention of 89.4% (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314954-fig-0005">5f</a>). The remarkable cycling performance of CF@ZnS@Na can be attributed to the synergy of the Na<sub>2</sub>S-reinforced interphase layer and the highly efficient NaZn<sub>13</sub> seeds. The high performance of CF@ZnS@Na reveals a promising practical application prospect for reliable energy storage.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202314954-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/19f7fd88-02ea-42c0-982f-32ea8b8f0a41/adfm202314954-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/19f7fd88-02ea-42c0-982f-32ea8b8f0a41/adfm202314954-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/70960f30-18a2-4223-8716-493478e7eeba/adfm202314954-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/19f7fd88-02ea-42c0-982f-32ea8b8f0a41/adfm202314954-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202314954-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202314954" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">The electrochemical performances of Na|NVP full cells. a) Rate performance of Na|NVP. b) Capacity-voltage profiles of CF@ZnS@Na|NVP at different current densities. c) Long-time cycling performance of Na|NVP cells at 20 C. d) The cycling performance of Na|NVP full cells with thick cathode (≈12&nbsp;mg cm<sup>−2</sup>) under harsh conditions with limited Na metal. e) Cycling performance of Na|NVP pouch cell with CF@ZnS@Na anode at 10 C. f) Cycling performance of Na|NVP pouch cell with thick cathode (≈12&nbsp;mg cm<sup>−2</sup>) and CF@ZnS@Na anode at 0.5 C charge and 1 C discharge. The inset shows the digital photograph of the pouch cell.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202314954-sec-0030">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202314954-sec-0030-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>A multifunctional CF@ZnS architecture has been facilely synthesized and rationally employed to construct CF@ZnS@Na anode. The attained Na<sub>2</sub>S-reinforced interphase layer and the effective volume accommodation cooperatively endow the fast Na<sup>+</sup> transfer kinetics, the highly reversible Na plating/stripping, and the uniform Na deposition. Hence, the Na symmetric cells with CF@ZnS@Na manifest ultrahigh rate capability (15&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup>) and ultralong cycling lifespan (5300 cycles). An ultralong cycling life of over 1390&nbsp;h still could be achieved for the CF@ZnS@Na even at the harsh test conditions of 5&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup> and 5 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>. The achieved Na|NVP full cell also shows ultrahigh rate performance (80 C) and extraordinarily ultralong lifespan cycling durability (2980 cycles). Impressively, the Na|NVP pouch cell delivers a remarkable long-term cycling sustainability over 1070 cycles with an extremely low capacity decay rate of 0.0088% per cycle even at 10 C. And the Na|NVP pouch cell with ultrathick cathode (≈12&nbsp;mg cm<sup>−2</sup>) also demonstrates a long lifespan cycling stability. Furthermore, long cycling life is also achieved for the Na|NVP full cell with CF@ZnS@Na even at a more realistic condition of a low negative-to-positive capacity (N/P) ratio of 2.3. This work enlightens a new strategy toward practical Na metal batteries.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202314954-sec-0040">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202314954-sec-0040-title">4 Experimental Section</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202314954-sec-0050">
            
            <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202314954-sec-0060">
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202314954-sec-0070">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adfm202314954-sec-0070-title"> Preparation of CF@ZnS and CF@ZnS@Na</h4>
                  
                  <p>The CF@ZnS were prepared with the CF@ZIF-8 precursor followed by the sulfurization treatment. Firstly, dimethylimidazole (243&nbsp;mg, Aladdin) and Zn(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub>·6H<sub>2</sub>O (110&nbsp;mg, Aladdin) were dissolved separately in methanol (100&nbsp;mL) to get transparent solutions A and B with magnetic stirring. After that, solution A was transferred rapidly into solution B preloaded with carbon fiber substrate, and the reaction was kept for 12&nbsp;h at room temperature under stirring. The obtained carbon fibers were washed with methanol three times before further drying at 80&nbsp;°C overnight and denoted as CF@ZIF-8. Then CF@ZIF-8 was immersed in ethanol (50&nbsp;mL) solution of thioacetamide (150&nbsp;mg) and heated at 80 °C for 8&nbsp;h. The sample was washed thoroughly with ethanol several times before further drying at 80&nbsp;°C overnight. The as-prepared sample was then annealed at 450&nbsp;°C for 4&nbsp;h under the inert atmosphere, which was denoted as CF@ZnS.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202314954-sec-0080">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adfm202314954-sec-0080-title"> Characterization and Electrochemical Measurement</h4>
                  
                  <p>The physical phase structure of samples was investigated on XRD (Bruker D8 Advance) with Cu Kα radiation (0.15406&nbsp;nm). The morphology observation of samples was carried out on SEM (JEOL 7800). XPS spectra were obtained on ThermoFisher ESCA 250XI using an Al Kα (<i>λ</i> = 0.83&nbsp;nm, <i>hυ</i> = 1486.7&nbsp;eV) with the C 1s neutral peak at 284.8&nbsp;eV as the reference. The TEM characterization was performed on a thermionic transmission electron microscope (JEM 2100 plus). CR2032-type asymmetric coin cells were assembled in an Ar-filled glovebox. The CF@ZnS@Na electrodes were achieved by the electrodeposition method with the CF@ZnS as the working electrode. For Na symmetric cells, two identical CF@ZnS@Na electrodes sandwiched with Celgard 2400 as the separator with 50&nbsp;µL of 1&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> in diglyme were used as electrolytes for both CE and symmetric cell tests. The Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (NVP) electrode was fabricated by the NVP powder (MTI Corporation), super P (MTI Corporation), and polyvinylidene fluoride (PVDF, MTI Corporation) in the weight ratio of 8:1:1 in N-methyl-2-pyrrolidone (NMP) with the Al foil as the current collector. The active material loading in the cathode was ≈4&nbsp;mg cm<sup>−2</sup>. The high mass loading NVP electrode was prepared by mixed NVP (MTI Corporation), super P (MTI Corporation), and polyvinylidene fluoride (MTI Corporation) in the weight ratio of 88:5:7 in N-methyl-2-pyrrolidone with the Al foil as the current collector, the active material loading in the final cathode was ≈12&nbsp;mg cm<sup>−2</sup>. The full cells were prepared using the CF@ZnS@Na anodes, NVP cathodes, and glass fiber (GF/D, Whatman) separators with 1.0&nbsp;M NaClO<sub>4</sub> in EC: DEC = 1:1 volume ratio with 5% FEC as the additives.</p>
                  </section>
               </section>
            </section>
         </section>
